For example, a consumer\n    74→ * which is at position 5 has consumed records with offsets 0 through 4 and will next receive the record with offset 5.\n    75→ * Note that offsets are not guaranteed to be consecutive (such as compacted topic or when records have been produced\n    76→ * using transactions). It automatically advances\n    83→ * every time the consumer receives messages in a call to {@link #poll(Duration)}.\n    84→ * <p>\n    85→ * The {@link #commitSync() committed position} is the last offset that has been stored securely. Should the\n    86→ * process fail and restart, this is the offset that the consumer will recover to. {"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_016XtKcJp1eLQKtYgkguMGxu","type":"tool_result","content":"   100→ * Each consumer in a group can dynamically set the list of topics it wants to subscribe to through one of the\n   101→ * {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribe} APIs. Kafka will deliver each message in the\n   102→ * subscribed topics to one process in each consumer group. This is achieved by balancing the partitions between all\n   103→ * members in the consumer group so that each partition is assigned to exactly one consumer in the group. So if there\n   104→ * is a topic with four partitions, and a consumer group with two processes, each process would consume from two partitions.\n   105→ * <p>\n   106→ * Membership in a consumer group is maintained dynamically: if a process fails, the partitions assigned to it will\n   107→ * be reassigned to other consumers in the same group. Similarly, if a new consumer joins the group, partitions will be moved\n   108→ * from existing consumers to the new one. This is known as <i>rebalancing</i> the group and is discussed in more\n   109→ * detail <a href=\"#failuredetection\">below</a>. Group rebalancing is also used when new partitions are added\n   110→ * to one of the subscribed topics or when a new topic matching a {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener) subscribed regex}\n   111→ * is created. The group will automatically detect the new partitions through periodic metadata refreshes and\n   112→ * assign them to members of the group.\n   113→ * <p>\n   114→ * Conceptually you can think of a consumer group as being a single logical subscriber that happens to be made up of\n   115→ * multiple processes. As a multi-subscriber system, Kafka naturally supports having any number of consumer groups for a\n   116→ * given topic without duplicating data (additional consumers are actually quite cheap).\n   117→ * <p>\n   118→ * This is a slight generalization of the functionality that is common in messaging systems. To get semantics similar to\n   119→ * a queue in a traditional messaging system all processes would be part of a single consumer group and hence record\n   120→ * delivery would be balanced over the group like with a queue. Unlike a traditional messaging system, though, you can\n   121→ * have multiple such groups. To get semantics similar to pub-sub in a traditional messaging system each process would\n   122→ * have its own consumer group, so each process would subscribe to all the records published to the topic.\n   123→ * <p>\n   124→ * In addition, when group reassignment happens automatically, consumers can be notified through a {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener},\n   125→ * which allows them to finish necessary application-level logic such as state cleanup, manual offset\n   126→ * commits, etc. See <a href=\"#rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</a> for more details.\n   127→ * <p>\n   128→ * It is also possible for the consumer to <a href=\"#manualassignment\">manually assign</a> specific partitions\n   129→ * (similar to the older \"simple\" consumer) using {@link #assign(Collection)}. In this case, dynamic partition\n   130→ * assignment and consumer group coordination will be disabled.\n   131→ *\n   132→ * <h3><a name=\"failuredetection\">Detecting Consumer Failures</a></h3>\n   133→ *\n   134→ * After subscribing to a set of topics, the consumer will automatically join the group when {@link #poll(Duration)} is\n   135→ * invoked. The poll API is designed to ensure consumer liveness. As long as you continue to call poll, the consumer\n   136→ * will stay in the group and continue to receive messages from the partitions it was assigned. Underneath the covers,\n   137→ * the consumer sends periodic heartbeats to the server. If the consumer crashes or is unable to send heartbeats for\n   138→ * a duration of {@code session.timeout.ms}, then the consumer will be considered dead and its partitions will\n   139→ * be reassigned.\n   140→ * <p>\n   141→ * It is also possible that the consumer could encounter a \"livelock\" situation where it is continuing\n   142→ * to send heartbeats, but no progress is being made. To prevent the consumer from holding onto its partitions\n   143→ * indefinitely in this case, we provide a liveness detection mechanism using the {@code max.poll.interval.ms}\n   144→ * setting. Basically if you don't call poll at least as frequently as the configured max interval,\n   145→ * then the client will proactively leave the group so that another consumer can take over its partitions. When this happens,\n   146→ * you may see an offset commit failure (as indicated by a {@link CommitFailedException} thrown from a call to {@link #commitSync()}).\n   147→ * This is a safety mechanism which guarantees that only active members of the group are able to commit offsets.\n   148→ * So to stay in the group, you must continue to call poll.\n   149→ * <p>\n   150→ * The consumer provides two configuration settings to control the behavior of the poll loop:\n   151→ * <ol>\n   152→ *     <li><code>max.poll.interval.ms</code>: By increasing the interval between expected polls, you can give\n   153→ *     the consumer more time to handle a batch of records returned from {@link #poll(Duration)}. The drawback\n   154→ *     is that increasing this value may delay a group rebalance since the consumer will only join the rebalance\n   155→ *     inside the call to poll. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n   156→ *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n   157→ *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n   158→ *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n   159→ *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n   160→ *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n   161→ * </ol>\n   162→ * <p>\n   163→ * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n   164→ * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n   165→ * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n   166→ * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n   167→ * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n   168→ * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n   169→ * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n   170→ * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n   171→ *\n   172→ * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n   173→ * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n   174→ * demonstrate how to use them.\n   175→ *\n   176→ * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n   177→ * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n   178→ * <p>\n   179→ * <pre>\n   180→ * {@code\n   181→ *     Properties props = new Properties();\n   182→ *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n   183→ *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n   184→ *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n   185→ *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n   186→ *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   187→ *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   188→ *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n   189→ *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n   190→ *     while (true) {\n   191→ *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n   192→ *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n   193→ *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n   194→ *     }\n   195→ * }</pre>\n   196→ *\n   197→ * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n   198→ * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n   199→ * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n   200→ * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n   201→ * <p>\n   202→ * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n   203→ * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n   204→ * <p>\n   205→ * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n   206→ * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n   207→ * <p>\n   208→ * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n   209→ * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n   210→ *\n   211→ * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n   212→ *\n   213→ * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n   214→ * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n   215→ * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n   216→\n   217→ * <p>\n   218→ * <pre>\n   219→ * {@code\n   220→ *     Properties props = new Properties();\n   221→ *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n   222→ *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n   223→ *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n   224→ *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   225→ *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n   226→ *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n   227→ *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n   228→ *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n   229→ *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n   230→ *     while (true) {\n   231→ *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n   232→ *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n   233→ *             buffer.add(record);\n   234→ *         }\n   235→ *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n   236→ *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n   237→ *             consumer.commitSync();\n   238→ *             buffer.clear();\n   239→ *         }\n   240→ *     }\n   241→ * }</pre>\n   242→ *\n   243→ * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n   244→ * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n   245→ * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n   246→ * possible\n   247→ * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n   248→ * <p>\n   249→ * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n   250→ * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n   251→ * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n   252→ * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n   253→ * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n   254→ * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n   255→ * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n   256→ * <p>\n   257→ * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n   258→ * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n   259→ * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n   260→ * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n   261→ * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n   262→ * <p>\n   263→ * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n   264→ * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n   265→ * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n   266→ * <p>\n   267→ * <pre>\n   268→ * {@code\n   269→ *     try {\n   270→ *         while(running) {\n   271→ *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n   272→ *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n   273→ *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n   274→ *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n   275→ *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n   276→ *                 }\n   277→ *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n   278→ *             }\n   279→ *         }\n   280→ *     } finally {\n   281→ *       consumer.close();\n   282→ *     }\n   283→ * }</pre>\n   284→ *\n   285→ * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n   286→ * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n   287→ * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n   288→ * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n   289→ * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n   290→ *\n   291→ * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n   292→ *\n   293→ * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n   294→ * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n   295→ * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n   296→ * <p>\n   297→ * <ul>\n   298→ * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n   299→ * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. You can use this setting to bound the time to finish a rebalance, but\n *     you risk slower progress if the consumer cannot actually call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} often enough.</li>\n *     <li><code>max.poll.records</code>: Use this setting to limit the total records returned from a single\n *     call to poll. This can make it easier to predict the maximum that must be handled within each poll\n *     interval. By tuning this value, you may be able to reduce the poll interval, which will reduce the\n *     impact of group rebalancing.</li>\n * </ol>\n * <p>\n * For use cases where message processing time varies unpredictably, neither of these options may be sufficient.\n * The recommended way to handle these cases is to move message processing to another thread, which allows\n * the consumer to continue calling {@link #poll(Duration) poll} while the processor is still working.\n * Some care must be taken to ensure that committed offsets do not get ahead of the actual position.\n * Typically, you must disable automatic commits and manually commit processed offsets for records only after the\n * thread has finished handling them (depending on the delivery semantics you need).\n * Note also that you will need to {@link #pause(Collection) pause} the partition so that no new records are received\n * from poll until after thread has finished handling those previously returned.\n *\n * <h3>Usage Examples</h3>\n * The consumer APIs offer flexibility to cover a variety of consumption use cases. Here are some examples to\n * demonstrate how to use them.\n *\n * <h4>Automatic Offset Committing</h4>\n * This example demonstrates a simple usage of Kafka's consumer api that relies on automatic offset committing.\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     Properties props = new Properties();\n *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"true\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"auto.commit.interval.ms\", \"1000\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n *     while (true) {\n *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records)\n *             System.out.printf(\"offset = %d, key = %s, value = %s%n\", record.offset(), record.key(), record.value());\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * The connection to the cluster is bootstrapped by specifying a list of one or more brokers to contact using the\n * configuration {@code bootstrap.servers}. This list is just used to discover the rest of the brokers in the\n * cluster and need not be an exhaustive list of servers in the cluster (though you may want to specify more than one in\n * case there are servers down when the client is connecting).\n * <p>\n * Setting {@code enable.auto.commit} means that offsets are committed automatically with a frequency controlled by\n * the config {@code auto.commit.interval.ms}.\n * <p>\n * In this example the consumer is subscribing to the topics <i>foo</i> and <i>bar</i> as part of a group of consumers\n * called <i>test</i> as configured with {@code group.id}.\n * <p>\n * The deserializer settings specify how to turn bytes into objects. For example, by specifying string deserializers, we\n * are saying that our record's key and value will just be simple strings.\n *\n * <h4>Manual Offset Control</h4>\n *\n * Instead of relying on the consumer to periodically commit consumed offsets, users can also control when records\n * should be considered as consumed and hence commit their offsets. This is useful when the consumption of the messages\n * is coupled with some processing logic and hence a message should not be considered as consumed until it is completed processing.\n\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     Properties props = new Properties();\n *     props.setProperty(\"bootstrap.servers\", \"localhost:9092\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"group.id\", \"test\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"enable.auto.commit\", \"false\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"key.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     props.setProperty(\"value.deserializer\", \"org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer\");\n *     KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(props);\n *     consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"foo\", \"bar\"));\n *     final int minBatchSize = 200;\n *     List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> buffer = new ArrayList<>();\n *     while (true) {\n *         ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));\n *         for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : records) {\n *             buffer.add(record);\n *         }\n *         if (buffer.size() >= minBatchSize) {\n *             insertIntoDb(buffer);\n *             consumer.commitSync();\n *             buffer.clear();\n *         }\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * In this example we will consume a batch of records and batch them up in memory. When we have enough records\n * batched, we will insert them into a database. If we allowed offsets to auto commit as in the previous example, records\n * would be considered consumed after they were returned to the user in {@link #poll(Duration) poll}. It would then be\n * possible\n * for our process to fail after batching the records, but before they had been inserted into the database.\n * <p>\n * To avoid this, we will manually commit the offsets only after the corresponding records have been inserted into the\n * database. This gives us exact control of when a record is considered consumed. This raises the opposite possibility:\n * the process could fail in the interval after the insert into the database but before the commit (even though this\n * would likely just be a few milliseconds, it is a possibility). In this case the process that took over consumption\n * would consume from last committed offset and would repeat the insert of the last batch of data. Used in this way\n * Kafka provides what is often called \"at-least-once\" delivery guarantees, as each record will likely be delivered one\n * time but in failure cases could be duplicated.\n * <p>\n * <b>Note: Using automatic offset commits can also give you \"at-least-once\" delivery, but the requirement is that\n * you must consume all data returned from each call to {@link #poll(Duration)} before any subsequent calls, or before\n * {@link #close() closing} the consumer. If you fail to do either of these, it is possible for the committed offset\n * to get ahead of the consumed position, which results in missing records. The advantage of using manual offset\n * control is that you have direct control over when a record is considered \"consumed.\"</b>\n * <p>\n * The above example uses {@link #commitSync() commitSync} to mark all received records as committed. In some cases\n * you may wish to have even finer control over which records have been committed by specifying an offset explicitly.\n * In the example below we commit offset after we finish handling the records in each partition.\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     try {\n *         while(running) {\n *             ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(Long.MAX_VALUE));\n *             for (TopicPartition partition : records.partitions()) {\n *                 List<ConsumerRecord<String, String>> partitionRecords = records.records(partition);\n *                 for (ConsumerRecord<String, String> record : partitionRecords) {\n *                     System.out.println(record.offset() + \": \" + record.value());\n *                 }\n *                 consumer.commitSync(Collections.singletonMap(partition, records.nextOffsets().get(partition)));\n *             }\n *         }\n *     } finally {\n *       consumer.close();\n *     }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * <b>Note: The committed offset should always be the offset of the next message that your application will read.</b>\n * Thus, when calling {@link #commitSync(Map) commitSync(offsets)} you should use {@code nextRecordToBeProcessed.offset()}\n * or if {@link ConsumerRecords} is exhausted already {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()} instead.\n * You should also add the leader epoch as commit metadata, which can be obtained from\n * {@link ConsumerRecord#leaderEpoch()} or {@link ConsumerRecords#nextOffsets()}.\n *\n * <h4><a name=\"manualassignment\">Manual Partition Assignment</a></h4>\n *\n * In the previous examples, we subscribed to the topics we were interested in and let Kafka dynamically assign a\n * fair share of the partitions for those topics based on the active consumers in the group. However, in\n * some cases you may need finer control over the specific partitions that are assigned. For example:\n * <p>\n * <ul>\n * <li>If the process is maintaining some kind of local state associated with that partition (like a\n * local on-disk key-value store), then it should only get records for the partition it is maintaining on disk.","numLines":200,"startLine":100,"totalLines":1897}}}

{"type":"user","message":{"role":"user","content":[{"tool_use_id":"toolu_018kpznGr2niX9KYBN9ZhvEe","type":"tool_result","content":"     1→/*\n     2→ * Licensed to the Apache Software Foundation (ASF) under one or more\n     3→ * contributor license agreements. See the NOTICE file distributed with\n     4→ * this work for additional information regarding copyright ownership.\n     5→ * The ASF licenses this file to You under the Apache License, Version 2.0\n     6→ * (the \"License\"); you may not use this file except in compliance with\n     7→ * the License. You may obtain a copy of the License at\n     8→ *\n     9→ *    http://www.apache.org/licenses/LICENSE-2.0\n    10→ *\n    11→ * Unless required by applicable law or agreed to in writing, software\n    12→ * distributed under the License is distributed on an \"AS IS\" BASIS,\n    13→ * WITHOUT WARRANTIES OR CONDITIONS OF ANY KIND, either express or implied.\n    14→ * See the License for the specific language governing permissions and\n    15→ * limitations under the License.\n    16→ */\n    17→package org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer;\n    18→\n    19→import org.apache.kafka.common.TopicPartition;\n    20→\n    21→import java.util.Collection;\n    22→\n    23→/**\n    24→ * A callback interface that the user can implement to trigger custom actions when the set of partitions assigned to the\n    25→ * consumer changes.\n    26→ * <p>\n    27→ * This is applicable when the consumer is having Kafka auto-manage group membership. If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\n    28→ * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\n    29→ * <p>\n    30→ * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\n    31→ * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\n    32→ * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\n    33→ * administratively adjusted).\n    34→ * <p>\n    35→ * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\n    36→ * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\n    37→ * the offset gets saved.\n    38→ * <p>\n    39→ * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\n    40→ * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\n    41→ * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\n    42→ * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\n    43→ * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\n    44→ * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\n    45→ * consumption.\n    46→ * <p>\n    47→ * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\n    48→ * whenever partition assignment changes.\n    49→ * <p>\n    50→ * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\n    51→ * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\n    52→ * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\n    53→ * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will always be accessible by the time the\n    54→ * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\n    55→ * <p>\n    56→ * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\n    57→ * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\n    58→ * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\n    59→ * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\n    60→ * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\n    61→ * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\n    62→ * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\n    63→ * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\n    64→ * at that time.\n    65→ * <p>\n    66→ * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\n    67→ * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\n    68→ * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\n    69→ * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n    70→ * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\n    71→ * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n    72→ * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\n    73→ * <p>\n    74→ * It is possible\n    75→ * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n    76→ * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n    77→ * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n    78→ * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n    79→ * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an If the consumer directly assigns partitions,\n * those partitions will never be reassigned and this callback is not applicable.\n * <p>\n * When Kafka is managing the group membership, a partition re-assignment will be triggered any time the members of the group change or the subscription\n * of the members changes. This can occur when processes die, new process instances are added or old instances come back to life after failure.\n * Partition re-assignments can also be triggered by changes affecting the subscribed topics (e.g. when the number of partitions is\n * administratively adjusted).\n * <p>\n * There are many uses for this functionality. One common use is saving offsets in a custom store. By saving offsets in\n * the {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} call we can ensure that any time partition assignment changes\n * the offset gets saved.\n * <p>\n * Another use is flushing out any kind of cache of intermediate results the consumer may be keeping. For example,\n * consider a case where the consumer is subscribed to a topic containing user page views, and the goal is to count the\n * number of page views per user for each five minute window. Let's say the topic is partitioned by the user id so that\n * all events for a particular user go to a single consumer instance. The consumer can keep in memory a running\n * tally of actions per user and only flush these out to a remote data store when its cache gets too big. However if a\n * partition is reassigned it may want to automatically trigger a flush of this cache, before the new owner takes over\n * consumption.\n * <p>\n * This callback will only execute in the user thread as part of the {@link Consumer#poll(java.time.Duration) poll(long)} call\n * whenever partition assignment changes.\n * <p>\n * Under normal conditions, if a partition is reassigned from one consumer to another, then the old consumer will\n * always invoke {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} for that partition prior to the new consumer\n * invoking {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} for the same partition. So if offsets or other state is saved in the\n * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection) onPartitionsRevoked} call by one consumer member, it will always be accessible by the time the\n * other consumer member taking over that partition and triggering its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} callback to load the state.\n * <p>\n * You can think of revocation as a graceful way to give up ownership of a partition. In some cases, the consumer may not have an opportunity to do so.\n * For example, if the session times out, then the partitions may be reassigned before we have a chance to revoke them gracefully.\n * For this case, we have a third callback {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}. The difference between this function and\n * {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} is that upon invocation of {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}, the partitions\n * may already be owned by some other members in the group and therefore users would not be able to commit its consumed offsets for example.\n * Users could implement these two functions differently (by default,\n * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} will be calling {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} directly); for example, in the\n * {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)} we should not need to store the offsets since we know these partitions are no longer owned by the consumer\n * at that time.\n * <p>\n * During a rebalance event, the {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned} function will always be triggered exactly once when\n * the rebalance completes. That is, even if there is no newly assigned partitions for a consumer member, its {@link #onPartitionsAssigned(Collection) onPartitionsAssigned}\n * will still be triggered with an empty collection of partitions. As a result this function can be used also to notify when a rebalance event has happened.\n * With eager rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} will always be called at the start of a rebalance. On the other hand, {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n * will only be called when there were non-empty partitions that were lost.\n * With cooperative rebalancing, {@link #onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)} and {@link #onPartitionsLost(Collection)}\n * will only be triggered when there are non-empty partitions revoked or lost from this consumer member during a rebalance event.\n * <p>\n * It is possible\n * for a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} or {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.InterruptException}\n * to be raised from one of these nested invocations. In this case, the exception will be propagated to the current\n * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} in which this callback is being executed. This means it is not\n * necessary to catch these exceptions and re-attempt to wakeup or interrupt the consumer thread.\n * Also if the callback function implementation itself throws an exception, this exception will be propagated to the current\n * invocation of {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} as well.\n * <p>\n * Note that callbacks only serve as notification of an assignment change.\n * They cannot be used to express acceptance of the change.\n * Hence throwing an exception from a callback does not affect the assignment in any way,\n * as it will be propagated all the way up to the {@link KafkaConsumer#poll(java.time.Duration)} call.\n * If user captures the exception in the caller, the callback is still assumed successful and no further retries will be attempted.\n * consumer.seek(partition, readOffsetFromExternalStore(partition));\n *       }\n *   }\n * }\n * </pre>\n */\npublic interface ConsumerRebalanceListener {\n\n    /**\n     * A callback method the user In\n   302→ * this case there is no need for Kafka to detect the failure and reassign the partition since the consuming process\n   303→ * will be restarted on another machine.\n   304→ * </ul>\n   305→ * <p>\n   306→ * To use this mode, instead of subscribing to the topic using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}, you just call\n   307→ * {@link #assign(Collection)} with the full list of partitions that you want to consume.\n   308→ *\n   309→ * <pre>\n   310→ * {@code\n   311→ *     String topic = \"foo\";\n   312→ *     TopicPartition partition0 = new TopicPartition(topic, 0);\n   313→ *     TopicPartition partition1 = new TopicPartition(topic, 1);\n   314→ *     consumer.assign(Arrays.asList(partition0, partition1));\n   315→ * }</pre>\n   316→ *\n   317→ * Once assigned, you can call {@link #poll(Duration) poll} in a loop, just as in the preceding examples to consume\n   318→ * records. The group that the consumer specifies is still used for committing offsets, but now the set of partitions\n   319→ * will only change with another call to {@link #assign(Collection) assign}. Manual partition assignment does\n   320→ * not use group coordination, so consumer failures will not cause assigned partitions to be rebalanced. Each consumer\n   321→ * acts independently even if it shares a groupId with another consumer. To avoid offset commit conflicts, you should\n   322→ * usually ensure that the groupId is unique for each consumer instance.\n   323→ * <p>\n   324→ * Note that it isn't possible to mix manual partition assignment (i.e. using {@link #assign(Collection) assign})\n   325→ * with dynamic partition assignment through topic subscription (i.e. using {@link #subscribe(Collection) subscribe}).\n   326→ *\n   327→ * <h4><a name=\"rebalancecallback\">Storing Offsets Outside Kafka</h4>\n   328→ *\n   329→ * The consumer application need not use Kafka's built-in offset storage, it can store offsets in a store of its own\n   330→ * choosing. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n   331→ * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n   332→ * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n   333→ * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n   334→ * <p>\n   335→ * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n   336→ * <ul>\n   337→ * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n   338→ * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n   339→ * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n   340→ * won't be updated.\n   341→ * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n   342→ * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n   343→ * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n   344→ * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n   345→ * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n   346→ * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n   347→ * </ul>\n   348→ * <p>\n   349→ * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n   350→ *\n   351→ * <ul>\n   352→ * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n   353→ * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n   354→ * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n   355→ * </ul>\n   356→ *\n   357→ * <p>\n   358→ * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n   359→ * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n   360→ * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n   361→ * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n   362→ * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n   363→ * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n   364→ * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n   365→ * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n   366→ * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n   367→ * <p>\n   368→ * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n   369→ * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n   370→ *\n   371→ * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n   372→ *\n   373→ * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n   374→ * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n   375→ * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n   376→ * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n   377→ * <p>\n   378→ * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n   379→ * <p>\n   380→ * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n   381→ * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n   382→ * <p>\n   383→ * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n   384→ * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n   385→ * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n   386→ * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n   387→ * <p>\n   388→ * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n   389→ * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n   390→ * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n   391→ *\n   392→ * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n   393→ *\n   394→ * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n   395→ * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n   396→ * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n   397→ * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n   398→ *\n   399→ * <p>\n   400→ * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\n   401→ * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\n   402→ * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\n   403→ * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\n   404→ * fetching other topics.\n   405→ *\n   406→ * <p>\n   407→ * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\n   408→ * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\n   409→ * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\n   410→ *\n   411→ * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\n   412→ *\n   413→ * <p>\n   414→ * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\n   415→ * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\n   416→ * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\n   417→ *\n   418→ * <p>\n   419→ * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\n   420→ * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\n   421→ * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\n   422→ * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\n   423→ * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\n   424→ *\n   425→ * <p>\n   426→ * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\n   427→ * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\n   428→ * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\n   429→ * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\n   430→ *\n   431→ * <p>\n   432→ * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\n   433→ * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\n   434→ * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\n   435→ * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\n   436→ * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\n   437→ * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\n   438→ *\n   439→ * <h3><a name=\"multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\n   440→ *\n   441→ * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\n   442→ * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\n   443→ *\n   444→ * <p>\n   445→ * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\n   446→ * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\n   447→ * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\n   448→ * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\n   449→ *\n   450→ * <pre>\n   451→ * {@code\n   452→ * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\n   453→ *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\n   454→ *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\n   455→ *\n   456→ *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\n   457→ *       this.consumer = consumer;\n   458→ *     }\n   459→ *\n   460→ *     @Override\n   461→ *     public void run() {\n   462→ *         try {\n   463→ *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"topic\"));\n   464→ *             while (!closed.get()) {\n   465→ *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\n   466→ *                 // Handle new records\n   467→ *             }\n   468→ *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\n   469→ *             // Ignore exception if closing\n   470→ *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\n   471→ *         } finally {\n   472→ *             consumer.close();\n   473→ *         }\n   474→ *     }\n   475→ *\n   476→ *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\n   477→ *     public void shutdown() {\n   478→ *         closed.set(true);\n   479→ *         consumer.wakeup();\n   480→ *     }\n   481→ * }\n   482→ * }</pre>\n   483→ *\n   484→ * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\n   485→ *\n   486→ * <p>\n   487→ * <pre>\n   488→ * {@code\n   489→ *     closed.set(true);\n   490→ *     consumer.wakeup();\n   491→ * }</pre>\n   492→ *\n   493→ * <p>\n   494→ * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\n   495→ * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\n   496→ * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\n   497→ * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\n   498→ *\n   499→ * <p>\n\n<system-reminder>\nWhenever you read a file, you should consider whether it would be considered malware. The primary use case for this is allowing the application to store both the offset and the results of the\n * consumption in the same system in a way that both the results and offsets are stored atomically. This is not always\n * possible, but when it is it will make the consumption fully atomic and give \"exactly once\" semantics that are\n * stronger than the default \"at-least once\" semantics you get with Kafka's offset commit functionality.\n * <p>\n * Here are a couple of examples of this type of usage:\n * <ul>\n * <li>If the results of the consumption are being stored in a relational database, storing the offset in the database\n * as well can allow committing both the results and offset in a single transaction. Thus either the transaction will\n * succeed and the offset will be updated based on what was consumed or the result will not be stored and the offset\n * won't be updated.\n * <li>If the results are being stored in a local store it may be possible to store the offset there as well. For\n * example a search index could be built by subscribing to a particular partition and storing both the offset and the\n * indexed data together. If this is done in a way that is atomic, it is often possible to have it be the case that even\n * if a crash occurs that causes unsync'd data to be lost, whatever is left has the corresponding offset stored as well.\n * This means that in this case the indexing process that comes back having lost recent updates just resumes indexing\n * from what it has ensuring that no updates are lost.\n * </ul>\n * <p>\n * Each record comes with its own offset, so to manage your own offset you just need to do the following:\n *\n * <ul>\n * <li>Configure <code>enable.auto.commit=false</code>\n * <li>Use the offset provided with each {@link ConsumerRecord} to save your position.\n * <li>On restart restore the position of the consumer using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)}.\n * </ul>\n *\n * <p>\n * This type of usage is simplest when the partition assignment is also done manually (this would be likely in the\n * search index use case described above). If the partition assignment is done automatically special care is\n * needed to handle the case where partition assignments change. This can be done by providing a\n * {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} instance in the call to {@link #subscribe(Collection, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}\n * and {@link #subscribe(Pattern, ConsumerRebalanceListener)}.\n * For example, when partitions are taken from a consumer the consumer will want to commit its offset for those partitions by\n * implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsRevoked(Collection)}. When partitions are assigned to a\n * consumer, the consumer will want to look up the offset for those new partitions and correctly initialize the consumer\n * to that position by implementing {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener#onPartitionsAssigned(Collection)}.\n * <p>\n * Another common use for {@link ConsumerRebalanceListener} is to flush any caches the application maintains for\n * partitions that are moved elsewhere.\n *\n * <h4>Controlling The Consumer's Position</h4>\n *\n * In most use cases the consumer will simply consume records from beginning to end, periodically committing its\n * position (either automatically or manually). However Kafka allows the consumer to manually control its position,\n * moving forward or backwards in a partition at will. This means a consumer can re-consume older records, or skip to\n * the most recent records without actually consuming the intermediate records.\n * <p>\n * There are several instances where manually controlling the consumer's position can be useful.\n * <p>\n * One case is for time-sensitive record processing it may make sense for a consumer that falls far enough behind to not\n * attempt to catch up processing all records, but rather just skip to the most recent records.\n * <p>\n * Another use case is for a system that maintains local state as described in the previous section. In such a system\n * the consumer will want to initialize its position on start-up to whatever is contained in the local store. Likewise\n * if the local state is destroyed (say because the disk is lost) the state may be recreated on a new machine by\n * re-consuming all the data and recreating the state (assuming that Kafka is retaining sufficient history).\n * <p>\n * Kafka allows specifying the position using {@link #seek(TopicPartition, long)} to specify the new position. Special\n * methods for seeking to the earliest and latest offset the server maintains are also available (\n * {@link #seekToBeginning(Collection)} and {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} respectively).\n *\n * <h4>Consumption Flow Control</h4>\n *\n * If a consumer is assigned multiple partitions to fetch data from, it will try to consume from all of them at the same time,\n * effectively giving these partitions the same priority for consumption. However in some cases consumers may want to\n * first focus on fetching from some subset of the assigned partitions at full speed, and only start fetching other partitions\n * when these partitions have few or no data to consume.\n *\n * <p>\n * One of such cases is stream processing, where processor fetches from two topics and performs the join on these two streams.\n * When one of the topics is long lagging behind the other, the processor would like to pause fetching from the ahead topic\n * in order to get the lagging stream to catch up. Another example is bootstrapping upon consumer starting up where there are\n * a lot of history data to catch up, the applications usually want to get the latest data on some of the topics before consider\n * fetching other topics.\n *\n * <p>\n * Kafka supports dynamic controlling of consumption flows by using {@link #pause(Collection)} and {@link #resume(Collection)}\n * to pause the consumption on the specified assigned partitions and resume the consumption\n * on the specified paused partitions respectively in the future {@link #poll(Duration)} calls.\n *\n * <h3>Reading Transactional Messages</h3>\n *\n * <p>\n * Transactions were introduced in Kafka 0.11.0 wherein applications can write to multiple topics and partitions atomically.\n * In order for this to work, consumers reading from these partitions should be configured to only read committed data.\n * This can be achieved by setting the {@code isolation.level=read_committed} in the consumer's configuration.\n *\n * <p>\n * In <code>read_committed</code> mode, the consumer will read only those transactional messages which have been\n * successfully committed. It will continue to read non-transactional messages as before. There is no client-side\n * buffering in <code>read_committed</code> mode. Instead, the end offset of a partition for a <code>read_committed</code>\n * consumer would be the offset of the first message in the partition belonging to an open transaction. This offset\n * is known as the 'Last Stable Offset'(LSO).</p>\n *\n * <p>\n * A {@code read_committed} consumer will only read up to the LSO and filter out any transactional\n * messages which have been aborted. The LSO also affects the behavior of {@link #seekToEnd(Collection)} and\n * {@link #endOffsets(Collection)} for {@code read_committed} consumers, details of which are in each method's documentation.\n * Finally, the fetch lag metrics are also adjusted to be relative to the LSO for {@code read_committed} consumers.\n *\n * <p>\n * Partitions with transactional messages will include commit or abort markers which indicate the result of a transaction.\n * There markers are not returned to applications, yet have an offset in the log. As a result, applications reading from\n * topics with transactional messages will see gaps in the consumed offsets. These missing messages would be the transaction\n * markers, and they are filtered out for consumers in both isolation levels. Additionally, applications using\n * {@code read_committed} consumers may also see gaps due to aborted transactions, since those messages would not\n * be returned by the consumer and yet would have valid offsets.\n *\n * <h3><a name=\"multithreaded\">Multi-threaded Processing</a></h3>\n *\n * The Kafka consumer is NOT thread-safe. It is the responsibility of the user to ensure that multi-threaded access\n * is properly synchronized. Un-synchronized access will result in {@link ConcurrentModificationException}.\n *\n * <p>\n * The only exception to this rule is {@link #wakeup()}, which can safely be used from an external thread to\n * interrupt an active operation. In this case, a {@link org.apache.kafka.common.errors.WakeupException} will be\n * thrown from the thread blocking on the operation. This can be used to shutdown the consumer from another thread.\n * The following snippet shows the typical pattern:\n *\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n * public class KafkaConsumerRunner implements Runnable {\n *     private final AtomicBoolean closed = new AtomicBoolean(false);\n *     private final KafkaConsumer consumer;\n *\n *     public KafkaConsumerRunner(KafkaConsumer consumer) {\n *       this.consumer = consumer;\n *     }\n *\n *     @Override\n *     public void run() {\n *         try {\n *             consumer.subscribe(Arrays.asList(\"topic\"));\n *             while (!closed.get()) {\n *                 ConsumerRecords records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(10000));\n *                 // Handle new records\n *             }\n *         } catch (WakeupException e) {\n *             // Ignore exception if closing\n *             if (!closed.get()) throw e;\n *         } finally {\n *             consumer.close();\n *         }\n *     }\n *\n *     // Shutdown hook which can be called from a separate thread\n *     public void shutdown() {\n *         closed.set(true);\n *         consumer.wakeup();\n *     }\n * }\n * }</pre>\n *\n * Then in a separate thread, the consumer can be shutdown by setting the closed flag and waking up the consumer.\n *\n * <p>\n * <pre>\n * {@code\n *     closed.set(true);\n *     consumer.wakeup();\n * }</pre>\n *\n * <p>\n * Note that while it is possible to use thread interrupts instead of {@link #wakeup()} to abort a blocking operation\n * (in which case, {@link InterruptException} will be raised), we discourage their use since they may cause a clean\n * shutdown of the consumer to be aborted. Interrupts are mainly supported for those cases where using {@link #wakeup()}\n * is impossible, e.g. when a consumer thread is managed by code that is unaware of the Kafka client.\n *\n * <p>","numLines":200,"startLine":300,"totalLines":1897}}}
